</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Information_theory" title="Template:Information theory"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Information_theory" title="Template talk:Information theory"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Information_theory" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Information theory"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Information theory</b> is the mathematical study of the <a href="/wiki/Quantification_(science)" title="Quantification (science)">quantification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Data_storage" title="Data storage">storage</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications" title="Telecommunications">communication</a> of a particular type of mathematically defined <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>. The field was established and formalized by <a href="/wiki/Claude_Shannon" title="Claude Shannon">Claude Shannon</a> in the 1940s,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> though early contributions were made in the 1920s through the works of <a href="/wiki/Harry_Nyquist" title="Harry Nyquist">Harry Nyquist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Hartley" title="Ralph Hartley">Ralph Hartley</a>. It is at the intersection of <a href="/wiki/Electronic_engineering" title="Electronic engineering">electronic engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neurobiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">electrical engineering</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>As a simple example, if one flips a fair coin and does not know the outcome (heads or tails), then they lack a certain amount of information. If one looks at the coin, they will know the outcome and gain that same amount of information. For a fair coin, the probability of either heads or tails is 1/2 and that amount of information can be expressed as <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle -\log _{2}(1/2)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mo>&#x2212;<!-- − --></mo>
        <msub>
          <mi>log</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mn>1</mn>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mo>/</mo>
        </mrow>
        <mn>2</mn>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle -\log _{2}(1/2)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/33e01293d03ce1ddcb4450bab08b3b31ef4a0cf1" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.518ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle -\log _{2}(1/2)}"></span> = 1 bit of information.
</p><p>A key measure in information theory is <a href="/wiki/Information_entropy" class="mw-redirect" title="Information entropy">information entropy</a>. Entropy is equal to the lack of information about a <a href="/wiki/Random_variable" title="Random variable">random variable</a> or the outcome of a <a href="/wiki/Random_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Random process">random process</a>. In the above coin flip example, the entropy in the case where you don't know the outcome is 1 bit. In the case where you do know the outcome, the entropy is zero. As another example, knowing the outcome of a <a href="/wiki/Fair_coin" title="Fair coin">fair</a> <a href="/wiki/Coin_flip" class="mw-redirect" title="Coin flip">coin flip</a> (which has two equally likely outcomes) provides less information (lower entropy, less uncertainty) than identifying the outcome from a roll of a <a href="/wiki/Dice" title="Dice">die</a> (which has six equally likely outcomes). A simple way to look at information entropy in bits is approximately the average minimum number of yes/no questions you need to ask in order to recover complete information. (Exact for 2<sup>n</sup> possibilities and converges to exact for infinite possibilities). For the coin flip example, you only need to ask one question, e.g. "Is it heads?". "Yes" means its heads, "No" means it's tails, and you have your one bit of complete information.
</p><p>Some other important measures in information theory are <a href="/wiki/Mutual_information" title="Mutual information">mutual information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Channel_capacity" title="Channel capacity">channel capacity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Error_exponent" title="Error exponent">error exponents</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Relative_entropy" class="mw-redirect" title="Relative entropy">relative entropy</a>. Important sub-fields of information theory include <a href="/wiki/Source_coding" class="mw-redirect" title="Source coding">source coding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algorithmic_complexity_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Algorithmic complexity theory">algorithmic complexity theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algorithmic_information_theory" title="Algorithmic information theory">algorithmic information theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Information-theoretic_security" title="Information-theoretic security">information-theoretic security</a>.
</p><p>Applications of fundamental topics of information theory include source coding/<a href="/wiki/Data_compression" title="Data compression">data compression</a> (e.g. for <a href="/wiki/ZIP_(file_format)" title="ZIP (file format)">ZIP files</a>), and channel coding/<a href="/wiki/Error_detection_and_correction" title="Error detection and correction">error detection and correction</a> (e.g. for <a href="/wiki/Digital_subscriber_line" title="Digital subscriber line">DSL</a>). Its impact has been crucial to the success of the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a> missions to deep space,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:22&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Compact_disc" title="Compact disc">compact disc</a>, the feasibility of mobile phones and the development of the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The theory has also found applications in other areas, including <a href="/wiki/Statistical_inference" title="Statistical inference">statistical inference</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cryptography" title="Cryptography">cryptography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neurobiology" class="mw-redirect" title="Neurobiology">neurobiology</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Spikes&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spikes-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perception</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Signal_processing" title="Signal processing">signal processing</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, the evolution<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and function<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> of molecular codes (<a href="/wiki/Bioinformatics" title="Bioinformatics">bioinformatics</a>), <a href="/wiki/Thermal_physics" title="Thermal physics">thermal physics</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Molecular_dynamics" title="Molecular dynamics">molecular dynamics</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quantum_computing" title="Quantum computing">quantum computing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information_retrieval" title="Information retrieval">information retrieval</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_(Information_Gathering)" class="mw-redirect" title="Intelligence (Information Gathering)">intelligence gathering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plagiarism_detection" class="mw-redirect" title="Plagiarism detection">plagiarism detection</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pattern_recognition" title="Pattern recognition">pattern recognition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anomaly_detection" title="Anomaly detection">anomaly detection</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the analysis of <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">music</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span title="Outer space">outer space</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the dimensionality of <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Overview">Overview</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Information_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Information theory studies the transmission, processing, extraction, and utilization of <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>. Abstractly, information can be thought of as the resolution of uncertainty. In the case of communication of information over a noisy channel, this abstract concept was formalized in 1948 by Claude Shannon in a paper entitled <i><a href="/wiki/A_Mathematical_Theory_of_Communication" title="A Mathematical Theory of Communication">A Mathematical Theory of Communication</a></i>, in which information is thought of as a set of possible messages, and the goal is to send these messages over a noisy channel, and to have the receiver reconstruct the message with low probability of error, in spite of the channel noise. Shannon's main result, the <a href="/wiki/Noisy-channel_coding_theorem" title="Noisy-channel coding theorem">noisy-channel coding theorem</a>, showed that, in the limit of many channel uses, the rate of information that is asymptotically achievable is equal to the channel capacity, a quantity dependent merely on the statistics of the channel over which the messages are sent.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Spikes&#95;9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spikes-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Coding theory is concerned with finding explicit methods, called <i>codes</i>, for increasing the efficiency and reducing the error rate of data communication over noisy channels to near the channel capacity. These codes can be roughly subdivided into data compression (source coding) and <a href="/wiki/Error-correction" class="mw-redirect" title="Error-correction">error-correction</a> (channel coding) techniques. In the latter case, it took many years to find the methods Shannon's work proved were possible.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A third class of information theory codes are <a href="/wiki/Cryptographic_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryptographic algorithm">cryptographic algorithms</a> (both <a href="/wiki/Code_(cryptography)" title="Code (cryptography)">codes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cipher" title="Cipher">ciphers</a>). Concepts, methods and results from coding theory and information theory are widely used in cryptography and <a href="/wiki/Cryptanalysis" title="Cryptanalysis">cryptanalysis</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> such as the <a href="/wiki/Ban_(unit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ban (unit)">unit ban</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Historical_background">Historical background</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Information_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Historical background"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_information_theory" title="History of information theory">History of information theory</a></div>
<p>The landmark event <i>establishing</i> the discipline of information theory and bringing it to immediate worldwide attention was the publication of Claude Shannon's classic paper "A Mathematical Theory of Communication" in the <i><a href="/wiki/Bell_System_Technical_Journal" class="mw-redirect" title="Bell System Technical Journal">Bell System Technical Journal</a></i> in July and October 1948. Historian <a href="/wiki/James_Gleick" title="James Gleick">James Gleick</a> rated the paper as the most important development of 1948, noting that the paper was "even more profound and more fundamental" than the <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistor</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGleick20113–4&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleick20113–4-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He came to be known as the "father of information theory".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Shannon outlined some of his initial ideas of information theory as early as 1939 in a letter to <a href="/wiki/Vannevar_Bush" title="Vannevar Bush">Vannevar Bush</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Prior to this paper, limited information-theoretic ideas had been developed at <a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs" title="Bell Labs">Bell Labs</a>, all implicitly assuming events of equal probability. Harry Nyquist's 1924 paper, <i>Certain Factors Affecting Telegraph Speed</i>, contains a theoretical section quantifying "intelligence" and the "line speed" at which it can be transmitted by a communication system, giving the relation <span class="texhtml"><i>W</i> = <i>K</i> log <i>m</i></span> (recalling the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a>), where <i>W</i> is the speed of transmission of intelligence, <i>m</i> is the number of different voltage levels to choose from at each time step, and <i>K</i> is a constant. Ralph Hartley's 1928 paper, <i>Transmission of Information</i>, uses the word <i>information</i> as a measurable quantity, reflecting the receiver's ability to distinguish one <a href="/wiki/Sequence_of_symbols" class="mw-redirect" title="Sequence of symbols">sequence of symbols</a> from any other, thus quantifying information as <span class="texhtml"><i>H</i> = log <i>S</i><sup><i>n</i></sup> = <i>n</i> log <i>S</i></span>, where <i>S</i> was the number of possible symbols, and <i>n</i> the number of symbols in a transmission. The unit of information was therefore the <a href="/wiki/Decimal_digit" class="mw-redirect" title="Decimal digit">decimal digit</a>, which since has sometimes been called the <a href="/wiki/Hartley_(unit)" title="Hartley (unit)">hartley</a> in his honor as a unit or scale or measure of information. <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a> in 1940 used similar ideas as part of the statistical analysis of the breaking of the German second world war <a href="/wiki/Cryptanalysis_of_the_Enigma" title="Cryptanalysis of the Enigma">Enigma</a> ciphers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Much of the mathematics behind information theory with events of different probabilities were developed for the field of <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamics" title="Thermodynamics">thermodynamics</a> by <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann" title="Ludwig Boltzmann">Ludwig Boltzmann</a> and <a href="/wiki/J._Willard_Gibbs" class="mw-redirect" title="J. Willard Gibbs">J. Willard Gibbs</a>. Connections between information-theoretic entropy and thermodynamic entropy, including the important contributions by <a href="/wiki/Rolf_Landauer" title="Rolf Landauer">Rolf Landauer</a> in the 1960s, are explored in <i><a href="/wiki/Entropy_in_thermodynamics_and_information_theory" title="Entropy in thermodynamics and information theory">Entropy in thermodynamics and information theory</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In Shannon's revolutionary and groundbreaking paper, the work for which had been substantially completed at Bell Labs by the end of 1944, Shannon for the first time introduced the qualitative and quantitative model of communication as a statistical process underlying information theory, opening with the assertion:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p>
<dl><dd>"<i>The fundamental problem of communication is that of reproducing at one point, either exactly or approximately, a message selected at another point.</i>"</dd></dl>
<p>With it came the ideas of: 
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Information_entropy" class="mw-redirect" title="Information entropy">information entropy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Redundancy_(information_theory)" title="Redundancy (information theory)">redundancy</a> of a source, and its relevance through the <a href="/wiki/Source_coding_theorem" class="mw-redirect" title="Source coding theorem">source coding theorem</a>;</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Mutual_information" title="Mutual information">mutual information</a>, and the channel capacity of a noisy channel, including the promise of perfect loss-free communication given by the noisy-channel coding theorem;</li>
<li>The practical result of the <a href="/wiki/Shannon%E2%80%93Hartley_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Shannon–Hartley law">Shannon–Hartley law</a> for the channel capacity of a <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_channel" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaussian channel">Gaussian channel</a>; as well as</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Bit" title="Bit">bit</a>—a new way of seeing the most fundamental unit of information.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Quantities_of_information">Quantities of information</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Information_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Quantities of information"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quantities_of_information" title="Quantities of information">Quantities of information</a></div>
<p>Information theory is based on <a href="/wiki/Probability_theory" title="Probability theory">probability theory</a> and statistics, where <a href="/wiki/Quantities_of_information" title="Quantities of information">quantified information</a> is usually described in terms of bits. Information theory often concerns itself with measures of information of the distributions associated with random variables. One of the most important measures is called <a href="/wiki/Entropy_(information_theory)" title="Entropy (information theory)">entropy</a>, which forms the building block of many other measures. Entropy allows quantification of measure of information in a single random variable.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Another useful concept is mutual information defined on two random variables, which quantifies the dependence between those variables, which is done by comparing the conditional and unconditional distributions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The former quantity is a property of the probability distribution of a random variable and gives a limit on the rate at which data generated by independent samples with the given distribution can be reliably compressed. The latter is a property of the joint distribution of two random variables and is the maximum rate of reliable communication across a noisy <a href="/wiki/Communication_channel" title="Communication channel">channel</a> in the limit of long block lengths, when the channel statistics are determined by the joint distribution.
</p><p>The choice of logarithmic base in the following formulae determines the <a href="/wiki/Units_of_measurement" class="mw-redirect" title="Units of measurement">unit</a> of information entropy that is used. A common unit of information is the bit or <a href="/wiki/Shannon_(unit)" title="Shannon (unit)">shannon</a>, based on the <a href="/wiki/Binary_logarithm" title="Binary logarithm">binary logarithm</a>. Other units include the <a href="/wiki/Nat_(unit)" title="Nat (unit)">nat</a>, which is based on the <a href="/wiki/Natural_logarithm" title="Natural logarithm">natural logarithm</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Deciban" class="mw-redirect" title="Deciban">decimal digit</a>, which is based on the <a href="/wiki/Common_logarithm" title="Common logarithm">common logarithm</a>.
</p><p>In what follows, an expression of the form <span class="texhtml"><i>p</i> log <i>p</i></span> is considered by convention to be equal to zero whenever <span class="texhtml"><i>p</i> = 0</span>. This is justified because <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle \lim _{p\rightarrow 0^{+}}p\log p=0}">
  <semantics>

</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Entropy_of_an_information_source">Entropy of an information source</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Information_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Entropy of an information source"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Based on the <a href="/wiki/Probability_mass_function" title="Probability mass function">probability mass function</a> of a source, the Shannon entropy <i>H</i>, in units of bits per symbol, is defined as the <a href="/wiki/Expected_value" title="Expected value">expected value</a> of the information content of the symbols.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CoverThomas&#95;Ch2&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CoverThomas_Ch2-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Arndt&#95;InfoTheory&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arndt_InfoTheory-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The amount of information conveyed by an individual source symbol <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle x_{i}}">

<p>A less probable symbol has a larger surprisal, meaning its occurrence provides more information.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MacKay&#95;Ch2&#95;37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacKay_Ch2-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The entropy <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle H}">

</p>

</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9e22bdeddcc03ace45a0db24c907bdafdac5a90c" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:55.211ex; height:5.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle H(X)\ =\ \mathbb {E} _{X}[I(x)]\ =\ \sum _{i}p_{i}I(p_{i})\ =\ -\sum _{i}p_{i}\log _{2}(p_{i})}"></span></dd></dl>
<p>Intuitively, the entropy <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle H(X)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>H</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle H(X)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bd232b6fb5ea803efc1154d2efb0c3fe00a4531b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:5.853ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle H(X)}"></span> of a <a href="/wiki/Discrete_random_variable" class="mw-redirect" title="Discrete random variable">discrete random variable</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i></span> is a measure of the amount of <i>uncertainty</i> associated with the value of <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle X}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>X</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle X}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/68baa052181f707c662844a465bfeeb135e82bab" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.98ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle X}"></span> when only its distribution is known.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CoverThomas&#95;Ch2&#95;35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CoverThomas_Ch2-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A high entropy indicates the outcomes are more evenly distributed, making the result harder to predict.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Schneider&#95;InfoTheoryPrimer&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schneider_InfoTheoryPrimer-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>For example, if one transmits 1000 bits (0s and 1s), and the value of each of these bits is known to the receiver (has a specific value with certainty) ahead of transmission, no information is transmitted. If, however, each bit is independently and equally likely to be 0 or 1, 1000 shannons of information (more often called bits) have been transmitted.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CoverThomas&#95;Ch1&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CoverThomas_Ch1-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Binary_entropy_plot.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Binary_entropy_plot.svg/250px-Binary_entropy_plot.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Binary_entropy_plot.svg/330px-Binary_entropy_plot.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Binary_entropy_plot.svg/500px-Binary_entropy_plot.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300" /></a><figcaption>The entropy of a <a href="/wiki/Bernoulli_trial" title="Bernoulli trial">Bernoulli trial</a> as a function of success probability, often called the <em><a href="/wiki/Binary_entropy_function" title="Binary entropy function">binary entropy function</a></em>, <span class="texhtml"><i>H</i><sub>b</sub>(<i>p</i>)</span>. The entropy is maximized at 1 bit per trial when the two possible outcomes are equally probable, as in an unbiased coin toss.</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Properties">Properties</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Information_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Properties"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A key property of entropy is that it is maximized when all the messages in the message space are <a href="/wiki/Equiprobable" class="mw-redirect" title="Equiprobable">equiprobable</a>. For a source with <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">n</span> possible symbols, where <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\textstyle p_{i}={\frac {1}{n}}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
        <msub>
          <mi>p</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mi>i</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mn>1</mn>
            <mi>n</mi>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\textstyle p_{i}={\frac {1}{n}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/07dd59ee1d01ed88696d468391a2e5b8c11987ad" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:6.979ex; height:3.343ex;" alt="{\textstyle p_{i}={\frac {1}{n}}}"></span> for all <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle i}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>i</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle i}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/add78d8608ad86e54951b8c8bd6c8d8416533d20" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.802ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle i}"></span>, the entropy is given by:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CoverThomas&#95;Ch2&#95;Theorem2.6.2&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CoverThomas_Ch2_Theorem2.6.2-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle H(X)=\log _{2}(n)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>H</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <msub>
          <mi>log</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>n</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle H(X)=\log _{2}(n)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ed28a012d3148e39270c94f7ba1cd03cdd5bb178" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:16.181ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle H(X)=\log _{2}(n)}"></span></dd></dl>
<p>This maximum value represents the most unpredictable state.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Reza&#95;Ch3&#95;39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reza_Ch3-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>For a source that emits a sequence of <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle N}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>N</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle N}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f5e3890c981ae85503089652feb48b191b57aae3" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.064ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle N}"></span> symbols that are <a href="/wiki/Independent_and_identically_distributed" class="mw-redirect" title="Independent and identically distributed">independent and identically distributed</a> (i.i.d.), the total entropy of the message is <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle N\cdot H}">

        <mi>H</mi>
      </mstyle>

    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle N\cdot H}</annotation>

If the source data symbols are identically distributed but not independent, the entropy of a message of length <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle N}">

  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/502ae1621cc63638c9722afe67afa4370b068041" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.806ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle N\cdot H}"></span>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CoverThomas&#95;Ch2&#95;Asymptotic&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CoverThomas_Ch2_Asymptotic-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-MacKay&#95;Ch4&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacKay_Ch4-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Units">Units</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Information_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Units"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The choice of the logarithmic base in the entropy formula determines the unit of entropy used:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MacKay&#95;Ch2&#95;37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacKay_Ch2-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Reza&#95;Ch3&#95;39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reza_Ch3-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>A <b><a href="/wiki/Base-2_logarithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Base-2 logarithm">base-2 logarithm</a></b> (as shown in the main formula) measures entropy in <b><a href="/wiki/Bit" title="Bit">bits</a></b> per symbol. This unit is also sometimes called the <b><a href="/wiki/Shannon_(unit)" title="Shannon (unit)">shannon</a></b> in honor of Claude Shannon.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CoverThomas&#95;Ch2&#95;35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CoverThomas_Ch2-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>A <b><a href="/wiki/Natural_logarithm" title="Natural logarithm">Natural logarithm</a></b> (base <i><a href="/wiki/E_(mathematical_constant)" title="E (mathematical constant)">e</a></i>) measures entropy in <b><a href="/wiki/Nat_(unit)" title="Nat (unit)">nats</a></b> per symbol. This is often used in theoretical analysis as it avoids the need for scaling constants (like ln 2) in derivations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Leung&#95;Nats&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leung_Nats-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Other bases are also possible. A <b>base-10 logarithm</b> measures entropy in decimal digits, or <b><a href="/wiki/Hartley_(unit)" title="Hartley (unit)">hartleys</a></b>, per symbol.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Arndt&#95;InfoTheory&#95;36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arndt_InfoTheory-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A <b>base-256 logarithm</b> measures entropy in <b><a href="/wiki/Byte" title="Byte">bytes</a></b> per symbol, since <span class="nowrap">2<sup>8</sup> = 256</span>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MacKay&#95;Ch2&#95;Bytes&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacKay_Ch2_Bytes-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Binary_Entropy_Function">Binary Entropy Function</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Information_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Binary Entropy Function"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The special case of information entropy for a random variable with two outcomes (a <a href="/wiki/Bernoulli_trial" title="Bernoulli trial">Bernoulli trial</a>) is the <b>binary entropy function</b>. This is typically calculated using a base-2 logarithm, and its unit is the <a href="/wiki/Shannon_(unit)" title="Shannon (unit)">shannon</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CoverThomas&#95;Binary&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CoverThomas_Binary-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> If one outcome has probability <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">p</span>, the other has probability <span class="texhtml"><i>1</i> − <i>p</i></span>. The entropy is given by:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MacKay&#95;Binary&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacKay_Binary-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle H_{\mathrm {b} }(p)=-p\log _{2}p-(1-p)\log _{2}(1-p)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <msub>
          <mi>H</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="normal">b</mi>
            </mrow>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>p</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mo>&#x2212;<!-- − --></mo>

          </mrow>
        </msub>

        <mi>p</mi>

  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/118d0a7ae01313db60949f1cea18711a934ea31e" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:39.318ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle H_{\mathrm {b} }(p)=-p\log _{2}p-(1-p)\log _{2}(1-p)}"></span></dd></dl>
<p>This function is depicted in the plot shown above, reaching its maximum of 1 bit when <span class="texhtml"><i>p</i> = 0.5</span>, corresponding to the highest uncertainty.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Joint_entropy">Joint entropy</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Information_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Joint entropy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <em><a href="/wiki/Joint_entropy" title="Joint entropy">joint entropy</a></em> of two discrete random variables <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>Y</i></span> is merely the entropy of their pairing: <span class="texhtml">(<i>X</i>, <i>Y</i>)</span>. This implies that if <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>Y</i></span> are <a href="/wiki/Statistical_independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Statistical independence">independent</a>, then their joint entropy is the sum of their individual entropies.
</p><p>For example, if <span class="texhtml">(<i>X</i>, <i>Y</i>)</span> represents the position of a chess piece—<span class="texhtml"><i>X</i></span> the row and <span class="texhtml"><i>Y</i></span> the column, then the joint entropy of the row of the piece and the column of the piece will be the entropy of the position of the piece.
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle H(X,Y)=\mathbb {E} _{X,Y}[-\log p(x,y)]=-\sum _{x,y}p(x,y)\log p(x,y)\,}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>Y</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <msub>

            <mi>X</mi>
            <mo>,</mo>
            <mi>Y</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
        <mo>&#x2212;<!-- − --></mo>
        <mi>log</mi>
        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
        <mi>p</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>y</mi>

</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a10572b1a21187833168e50a998f66856658674d" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -3.338ex; width:56.163ex; height:5.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle H(X,Y)=\mathbb {E} _{X,Y}[-\log p(x,y)]=-\sum _{x,y}p(x,y)\log p(x,y)\,}"></span></dd></dl>
<p>Despite similar notation, joint entropy should not be confused with <em><a href="/wiki/Cross-entropy" title="Cross-entropy">cross-entropy</a></em>.
</p><p>The join entropy of <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle n}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>n</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.395ex; height:1.676ex;" alt="{\displaystyle n}"></span> discrete random variables <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle X^{n}\triangleq (X_{1},X_{2},\ldots ,X_{n})}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <msup>
          <mi>X</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mi>n</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msup>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Conditional_entropy_(equivocation)"><span id="Conditional_entropy_.28equivocation.29"></span>Conditional entropy (equivocation)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Information_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Conditional entropy (equivocation)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <em><a href="/wiki/Conditional_entropy" title="Conditional entropy">conditional entropy</a></em> or <i>conditional uncertainty</i> of <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i></span> given random variable <span class="texhtml"><i>Y</i></span> (also called the <i>equivocation</i> of <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i></span> about <span class="texhtml"><i>Y</i></span>) is the average conditional entropy over <span class="texhtml"><i>Y</i></span>:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEAsh1990&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsh1990-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle H(X|Y)=\mathbb {E} _{Y}[H(X|y)]=-\sum _{y\in Y}p(y)\sum _{x\in X}p(x|y)\log p(x|y)=-\sum _{x,y}p(x,y)\log p(x|y).}">

<p>Because entropy can be conditioned on a random variable or on that random variable being a certain value, care should be taken not to confuse these two definitions of conditional entropy, the former of which is in more common use. A basic property of this form of conditional entropy is that:
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Mutual_information_(transinformation)"><span id="Mutual_information_.28transinformation.29"></span>Mutual information (transinformation)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Information_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Mutual information (transinformation)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Mutual_information" title="Mutual information">Mutual information</a></i> measures the amount of information that can be obtained about one random variable by observing another. It is important in communication where it can be used to maximize the amount of information shared between sent and received signals. The mutual information of <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i></span> relative to <span class="texhtml"><i>Y</i></span> is given by:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle I(X;Y)=\mathbb {E} _{X,Y}[SI(x,y)]=\sum _{x,y}p(x,y)\log {\frac {p(x,y)}{p(x)\,p(y)}}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>I</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo>;</mo>
        <mi>Y</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <msub>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mi mathvariant="double-struck">E</mi>
          </mrow>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mi>X</mi>
            <mo>,</mo>
            <mi>Y</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
        <mi>S</mi>
        <mi>I</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>y</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <munder>
          <mo>&#x2211;<!-- ∑ --></mo>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mi>x</mi>
            <mo>,</mo>
            <mi>y</mi>
          </mrow>
        </munder>
        <mi>p</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>y</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mi>log</mi>
        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mrow>
              <mi>p</mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
              <mi>x</mi>
              <mo>,</mo>
              <mi>y</mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
            </mrow>
            <mrow>
              <mi>p</mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
              <mi>x</mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
              <mspace width="thinmathspace" />
              <mi>p</mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
              <mi>y</mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
            </mrow>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle I(X;Y)=\mathbb {E} _{X,Y}[SI(x,y)]=\sum _{x,y}p(x,y)\log {\frac {p(x,y)}{p(x)\,p(y)}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7d86fba391a52b75e1cf36dae6bfaa1ccbccf461" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -3.338ex; width:51.804ex; height:7.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle I(X;Y)=\mathbb {E} _{X,Y}[SI(x,y)]=\sum _{x,y}p(x,y)\log {\frac {p(x,y)}{p(x)\,p(y)}}}"></span></dd></dl>
<p>where <span class="texhtml">SI</span> (<i>S</i>pecific mutual Information) is the <a href="/wiki/Pointwise_mutual_information" title="Pointwise mutual information">pointwise mutual information</a>.
</p><p>A basic property of the mutual information is that:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle I(X;Y)=H(X)-H(X|Y).\,}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>I</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo>;</mo>
        <mi>Y</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mi>H</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo>&#x2212;<!-- − --></mo>
        <mi>H</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
        </mrow>
        <mi>Y</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo>.</mo>
        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle I(X;Y)=H(X)-H(X|Y).\,}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ff968f71052c9abe1da3e3e414327077de6f2721" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:28.867ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle I(X;Y)=H(X)-H(X|Y).\,}"></span></dd></dl>
<p>That is, knowing <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\textstyle Y}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>Y</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\textstyle Y}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6222becc4f0c5effa012e5335b170575fdbbaad3" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.171ex; width:1.773ex; height:2.009ex;" alt="{\textstyle Y}"></span>, we can save an average of <span class="texhtml"><i>I</i>(<i>X</i>; <i>Y</i>)</span> bits in encoding <i><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle X}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>X</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle X}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/68baa052181f707c662844a465bfeeb135e82bab" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.98ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle X}"></span></i> compared to not knowing <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle Y}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>Y</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Y}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/961d67d6b454b4df2301ac571808a3538b3a6d3f" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.171ex; width:1.773ex; height:2.009ex;" alt="{\displaystyle Y}"></span>.
</p><p>Mutual information is <a href="/wiki/Symmetric_function" title="Symmetric function">symmetric</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle I(X;Y)=I(Y;X)=H(X)+H(Y)-H(X,Y).\,}">

    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle I(X;Y)=I(Y;X)=H(X)+H(Y)-H(X,Y).\,}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d8c8ca328d60dbd8eaab8833e9ec1e882daaa3b6" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:48.608ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle I(X;Y)=I(Y;X)=H(X)+H(Y)-H(X,Y).\,}"></span></dd></dl>
<p>Mutual information can be expressed as the average Kullback–Leibler divergence (information gain) between the <a href="/wiki/Posterior_probability" title="Posterior probability">posterior probability distribution</a> of <i><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle X}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>X</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle X}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/68baa052181f707c662844a465bfeeb135e82bab" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.98ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle X}"></span></i> given the value of <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\textstyle Y}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>Y</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\textstyle Y}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6222becc4f0c5effa012e5335b170575fdbbaad3" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.171ex; width:1.773ex; height:2.009ex;" alt="{\textstyle Y}"></span> and the <a href="/wiki/Prior_probability" title="Prior probability">prior distribution</a> on <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle X}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>X</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle X}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/68baa052181f707c662844a465bfeeb135e82bab" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.98ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle X}"></span>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle I(X;Y)=\mathbb {E} _{p(y)}[D_{\mathrm {KL} }(p(X|Y=y)\|p(X))].}">

  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d0d90fc72e685981072e77a3933c7ad6c4c83d13" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:41.539ex; height:3.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle I(X;Y)=\mathbb {E} _{p(y)}[D_{\mathrm {KL} }(p(X|Y=y)\|p(X))].}"></span></dd></dl>
<p>In other words, this is a measure of how much, on the average, the probability distribution on <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle X}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>X</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle X}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/68baa052181f707c662844a465bfeeb135e82bab" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.98ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle X}"></span> will change if we are given the value of <i><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\textstyle Y}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>Y</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\textstyle Y}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6222becc4f0c5effa012e5335b170575fdbbaad3" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.171ex; width:1.773ex; height:2.009ex;" alt="{\textstyle Y}"></span></i>. This is often recalculated as the divergence from the product of the marginal distributions to the actual joint distribution:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle I(X;Y)=D_{\mathrm {KL} }(p(X,Y)\|p(X)p(Y)).}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>I</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo>;</mo>
        <mi>Y</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <msub>
          <mi>D</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

              <mi mathvariant="normal">K</mi>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">L</mi>
            </mrow>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>

        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>Y</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#x2016;<!-- ‖ --></mo>
        <mi>p</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>

        <mi>p</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>

    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle I(X;Y)=D_{\mathrm {KL} }(p(X,Y)\|p(X)p(Y)).}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/03b108331b20bad2137992fa16f9fdd48906072d" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:36.424ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle I(X;Y)=D_{\mathrm {KL} }(p(X,Y)\|p(X)p(Y)).}"></span></dd></dl>
<p>Mutual information is closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Likelihood-ratio_test" title="Likelihood-ratio test">log-likelihood ratio test</a> in the context of contingency tables and the <a href="/wiki/Multinomial_distribution" title="Multinomial distribution">multinomial distribution</a> and to <a href="/wiki/Pearson%27s_chi-squared_test" title="Pearson&#39;s chi-squared test">Pearson's χ<sup>2</sup> test</a>: mutual information can be considered a statistic for assessing independence between a pair of variables, and has a well-specified asymptotic distribution.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Kullback–Leibler_divergence_(information_gain)"><span id="Kullback.E2.80.93Leibler_divergence_.28information_gain.29"></span>Kullback–Leibler divergence (information gain)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Information_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Kullback–Leibler divergence (information gain)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Kullback%E2%80%93Leibler_divergence" title="Kullback–Leibler divergence">Kullback–Leibler divergence</a></i> (or <i>information divergence</i>, <i>information gain</i>, or <i>relative entropy</i>) is a way of comparing two distributions: a "true" <a href="/wiki/Probability_distribution" title="Probability distribution">probability distribution</a> <span class="nowrap">&#8288;<span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle p(X)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>p</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle p(X)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f7425278aab7b6ceb8ddb54fa5ce71d2cf52d28f" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:5.048ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle p(X)}"></span>&#8288;</span>, and an arbitrary probability distribution <span class="nowrap">&#8288;<span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle q(X)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>q</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle q(X)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/352e294916cf0c2b425f097071daf0da1b47123b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.859ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle q(X)}"></span>&#8288;</span>. If we compress data in a manner that assumes <span class="nowrap">&#8288;<span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle q(X)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>q</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle q(X)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/352e294916cf0c2b425f097071daf0da1b47123b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.859ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle q(X)}"></span>&#8288;</span> is the distribution underlying some data, when, in reality, <span class="nowrap">&#8288;<span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle p(X)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>p</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle p(X)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f7425278aab7b6ceb8ddb54fa5ce71d2cf52d28f" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:5.048ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle p(X)}"></span>&#8288;</span> is the correct distribution, the Kullback–Leibler divergence is the number of average additional bits per datum necessary for compression. It is thus defined
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle D_{\mathrm {KL} }(p(X)\|q(X))=\sum _{x\in X}-p(x)\log {q(x)}\,-\,\sum _{x\in X}-p(x)\log {p(x)}=\sum _{x\in X}p(x)\log {\frac {p(x)}{q(x)}}.}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <msub>
